The Grandmother's Inheritance: Case Study of Solitary Testimony
Case study of grandmother inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the incident of the grandmother's inheritance under Abu Bakr and 'Umar to derive the principle that acceptance of solitary testimony is upheld after corroboration, reinforcing the Hanafi view on ahad hadith.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr accepted the solitary report of Mughira after confirmation, proving that one witness suffices with ihtiyat.

This lesson focuses on one specific historical incident: the grandmother who came to Abu Bakr seeking her inheritance, and later to 'Umar. The narration shows how the Sahaba handled solitary reports about a ruling from the Prophet. Abu Bakr initially did not know of a ruling, so he consulted the people. Mughira ibn Shu'ba testified that the Prophet gave the grandmother a sixth. Abu Bakr asked for another witness, and when Muhammad ibn Maslama confirmed, he implemented the ruling. This case illustrates that solitary testimony is accepted, but extra caution (ihtiyat) may be exercised. The lesson will dissect this event and its usul implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration carefully
Read the section beginning with 'Qabisa ibn Dhu'ayb said: The grandmother came to Abu Bakr...' until the end of the grandmother's case, including the footnote about ihtiyat.
Pay attention to Abu Bakr's initial response and his request for a second witness.
2
Identify the legal issue
The legal issue is the share of the grandmother in inheritance, for which there was no clear text in the Quran or known Sunnah. Abu Bakr had to seek knowledge.
This demonstrates that the Sahaba relied on human reports when divine texts were silent.
3
Trace the process of acceptance
Abu Bakr asked the people, Mughira testified, Abu Bakr asked for a second witness, Muhammad confirmed, then Abu Bakr implemented. Notice that the first testimony was not rejected; it was corroborated.
The request for a second witness was for extra certainty, not because the first was insufficient.
4
Extract the usul principle
The principle derived is that a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) from a reliable narrator is acceptable, and requesting additional witnesses is a matter of ihtiyat, not a condition of validity.
This is the Hanafi stance: a single trustworthy narrator suffices to establish a ruling, though caution may lead to seeking corroboration.
5
Compare with 'Umar's ruling
The second part of the narration shows 'Umar also ruling based on what he knew about the sixth, but he ruled without seeking additional testimony, indicating that when the report is known and accepted, no further action is needed.
This shows consistency in accepting solitary reports.

قلت سائد : الشَّفٌ : هو الربح والزيادة. النهاية ٤٨٦/٢]
ومن غريبهم عبيد الله بن عبد الخالق الأنصاري عن ابن عمر سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : مَن يذهب بكتابي إلى طاغية الروم وله الجنة، فقام رجل يُدعى عبيد الله بن عبد الخالق الأنصاري، فقال: أنا أذهب به». الحديث، أخرجه في «جامع المسانيد».
في
(۱) قوله : وكذلك أصحابه عملوا بالآحاد عن قبيصة بن ذؤيب قال: «جاءت الجدة إلى أبي بكر، فسألت ميراثها، فقال: ما لك في كتاب الله شيء، وما علمتُ لكِ سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم شيئاً، فارجعي حتى أسأل الناس، فسأل الناس، فقال المغيرة بن شعبة: حضرت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطاها السدس، فقال : هل معك غيرك؟ فقام محمد بن مسلمة الأنصاري، فقال مثل ما قال المغيرة بن شعبة، فأنفذه لها أبو بكر.
ثم جاءت الجدة الأخرى إلى عمر بن الخطاب فسألته ميراثها، فقال: ما لك كتاب الله شيء، ولكن هو ذاك السدس فإن اجتمعتما فهو بينكما، وأيكما خلت :به فهو لها». رواه الخمسة إلا النسائي، وصححه الترمذي.
وأخرج البخاري عن بجالةَ بن عَبَدَة : أتانا كتاب عمر قبل موته بسنة: فرقوا بين كل ذي رحم محرم من المجوس، ولم يكن عمر أخذ الجزية من المجوس حتى شهد عبد الرحمن بن عوف أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أخذها من مجوس هَجَر». وعن أبي هريرة أن فاطمة رضي الله عنها قالت لأبي بكر : مَن يرتُكَ إِذا مِتَّ، قال : ولدي وأهلي قالت فما لنا لا نرتُ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم؟ قال: سمعت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «إن النبي لا يُوَرَّث». الحديث رواه أحمد والترمذي وصححه.
و
وعن فريعة بنت مالك قالت : خرج زوجي في طلب أعلاج له، فأدركهم بطرف
=
القدوم ، فقتلوه ، فأتاني نعيه وأنا في دارٍ شاسعة من دور أهلي، فأتيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فذكرت ذلك له، فقلت: إن نعي زوجي أتاني في دار شاسعة من دور أهلي، ولم يدع نفقةً ولا مالاً ،ورثته وليس المسكن له، فلو تحولت إلى أهلي وإخوتي لكان أرفق بي في بعض شأني، قال: تحولي، فلما خرجتُ إلى المسجد أو إلى الحجرة دعاني أو أمَرَ بي فدعيت فقال امكثي في بيتك الذي أتاك فيه نعي زوجك حتى يبلغ الكتاب أجله ، قالت : فاعتددت فيه أربعة أشهر وعشراً. قالت فأرسل إليَّ عثمان فأخبرته، فأخذ به». رواه الخمسة وصححه الترمذي، ولم يذكر النسائي وابن ماجه إرسال عثمان.
(۱) قوله : ذكر محمد في هذا غير حديث في كتاب الاستحسان : قلت: لفظه فيه : ألا ترى أن أبا بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه شهد عنده المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطى الجدةَ أم الأم السدس ، فقال : ائتِ بشاهد آخر، فجاء محمد بن مسلمة، فشهد على مثل شهادته، فأعطى أبو بكر الجدة السدس.
وعمر شهد عنده أبو موسى أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «إذا استأذن
أحدكم ثلاثاً، فلم يُؤذن له فليرجع ، فقال : ائتِ معك بشاهد على ذلك. فهذا أفضل للاحتياط والواحد يجزىء، ألا ترى أن عمر عمر قبل شهادة عبد الرحمن بن عوف، شهد عنده وحده أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذكر عنده المجوس، فقال: سنوا بهم سنة أهل الكتاب في أخذ الخراج، فأجاز عمر قوله
وحده.
و
وأجاز عمر قول عبد الرحمن بن عوف في الطاعون حين أراد أن يدخل الشام، وكان بها الطاعون، فاستشار عمرُ في الدخول، فأشار إليه بعض المهاجرين بالدخول، وقال أبو عبيدة بن الجراح : يا أمير المؤمنين أتفر من قدر الله؟! وقال قوم من أهل مكة: لا تدخل، فجاء عبد الرحمن بن عوف فقال : إني سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «إذا وقع هذا الرجزُ بأَرض : فلا تدخلوا عليه، وإذا وقع
And this is more than can be counted and more famous than to be hidden. God and likewise His Companions, may God be pleased with them, acted upon isolated hadith (al-ahad) and argued with them. And I said Sa'id: Al-Shaff: is profit and increase. [Al-Nihaya 2/486]. And among their strange [reports] is 'Ubaydullah ibn 'Abd al-Khaliq al-Ansari from Ibn 'Umar: I heard the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, say: 'Who will take my letter to the tyrant of Rum and have Paradise?' So a man called 'Ubaydullah ibn 'Abd al-Khaliq al-Ansari stood up and said: I will take it.' The hadith is recorded in Jami' al-Masanid.
(1) His saying: Likewise His Companions acted upon isolated hadith. From Qabisa ibn Dhu'ayb who said: The grandmother came to Abu Bakr and asked about her inheritance. He said: You have nothing in the Book of God, and I do not know for you anything from the Sunnah of the Messenger of God. So go back until I ask the people. He asked the people, and Mughira ibn Shu'ba said: I witnessed the Messenger of God give her a sixth. He said: Do you have another witness? Then Muhammad ibn Maslama al-Ansari stood up and said what Mughira ibn Shu'ba said, so Abu Bakr implemented it for her. Then the other grandmother came to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and asked him about her inheritance. He said: You have nothing in the Book of God, but that is the sixth. If you are together, it is between you, and whichever of you is alone gets it. Recorded by the five except al-Nasa'i, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi. And al-Bukhari narrated from Bajala ibn 'Abada: The letter of 'Umar came to us a year before his death: Separate every mahram relative from the Magians. And 'Umar had not taken jizya from the Magians until 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf testified that the Messenger of God took it from the Magians of Hajar. And from Abu Hurayra that Fatima, may God be pleased with her, said to Abu Bakr: Who will inherit you when you die? He said: My children and my family. She said: Why don't we inherit the Prophet? He said: I heard the Prophet say: 'The Prophet is not inherited.' The hadith is recorded by Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi and authenticated. And from Furay'a bint Malik who said: My husband went out seeking some slaves of his, and he caught up with them at the edge of al-Qadum, and they killed him. Then the news of his death came to me while I was in a faraway house among my family. So I came to the Prophet and mentioned that to him. I said: The news of my husband's death came to me in a faraway house among my family, and he left no maintenance or wealth; I inherited him, but the house is not his. So if I move to my family and brothers, it would be more convenient for me in some of my affairs. He said: Move. When I went out to the mosque or to the room, he called me or ordered that I be called, and I was called, and he said: Stay in your house where the news of your husband's death came to you until the book reaches its term. She said: So I observed the waiting period there for four months and ten days. She said: Then 'Uthman sent for me and I told him, and he adopted it. Recorded by the five and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi; al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah did not mention the sending of 'Uthman. (1) His saying: Muhammad mentioned in this [book] other than a hadith in Kitab al-Istihsan. I say: Its wording in it: Do you not see that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, had Mughira ibn Shu'ba testify before him that the Prophet gave the grandmother the mother of the mother a sixth, so he said: Bring another witness. Then Muhammad ibn Maslama came and testified similarly, so Abu Bakr gave the grandmother the sixth. And 'Umar had Abu Musa testify before him that the Messenger of God said: 'When one of you asks permission three times and is not permitted, let him go back.' So he said: Bring a witness with you on that. So this is better for precaution, and one suffices. Do you not see that 'Umar accepted the testimony of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf alone when he testified before him that the Messenger of God mentioned the Magians in his presence and said: Treat them according to the practice of the People of the Book in taking the kharaj. So 'Umar accepted his statement alone. And 'Umar accepted the statement of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf concerning the plague when he wanted to enter Syria while the plague was there. So 'Umar consulted about entering, and some of the Muhajirun indicated entering, and Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah said: O Commander of the Faithful, do you flee from the decree of God? And some people from Mecca said: Do not enter. Then 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf came and said: I heard the Messenger of God say: 'When this affliction occurs in a land, do not enter it; and when it occurs
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage provides extensive historical evidence that the Companions (Sahaba) of the Prophet ﷺ acted upon isolated hadith (akhbar al-ahad). The author first notes that such examples are too numerous and too famous to require exhaustive enumeration. The core argument is: if the Sahaba themselves accepted single-person reports in matters of law (such as inheritance, jizya, and waiting period), then later scholars are justified in doing so. The examples cited include: (1) Abu Bakr accepting the testimony of Mughira ibn Shu'ba and Muhammad ibn Maslama regarding the grandmother's sixth share, initially requiring two witnesses but ultimately acting upon a single report combined with another; (2) 'Umar accepting the testimony of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf alone regarding the Magians' jizya; (3) 'Umar accepting 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf's solitary report about the plague and the prohibition of entering an afflicted land. Additionally, the story of Furay'a bint Malik illustrates the Prophet's own ruling based on a single narration. All these examples demonstrate that the Sahaba did not require multiple witnesses or chains for every report; rather, they acted upon ahad hadith when they deemed them reliable. The footnote from Kitab al-Istihsan further elaborates that 'Umar's request for additional witnesses was for precaution (ihtiyat), not because a single witness was insufficient. Thus, the Hanafi position that ahad hadith are valid and binding is firmly rooted in the practice of the early community.

Key Terms
الآحاد (al-ahad): isolated or single-narrator hadith, as opposed to mutawatir
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): a method of legal reasoning preferring a ruling due to equity or necessity, often based on a hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Bakr initially ask for a second witness from Mughira?
A) Because he did not trust Mughira.
B) Because Islamic law requires two witnesses for all matters.
C) For extra caution (ihtiyat), not because a single witness was insufficient.
D) Because the narrator was from a different tribe.

Key Takeaway
The grandmother case demonstrates that solitary testimony is accepted in Hanafi usul, and any request for additional witnesses is a matter of caution, not a condition of validity.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
6 min
'Umar's Acceptance of Solitary Testimony: Magians and Plague
Case studies of 'Umar's acceptance of solitary testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze two incidents from 'Umar's caliphate where he accepted solitary testimony from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf and derive the Hanafi principle that a single reliable narrator's report is binding.

Core Idea
'Umar accepted solitary testimony without demanding a second witness, establishing that a single reliable report is sufficient evidence.

This lesson examines two specific incidents during 'Umar ibn al-Khattab's caliphate: his acceptance of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf's solitary testimony regarding the jizya from Magians, and his acceptance of the same companion's solitary report about the plague in Syria. In both cases, 'Umar did not request a second witness. The text uses these examples to reinforce the Hanafi position that a solitary report from a trustworthy source is sufficient for legal action. The lesson also contrasts with the earlier case of Abu Bakr where a second witness was sought for caution, highlighting that the principle is not rigid.

Guided Path
1
Read the two incidents
Locate the sections: 'Umar and the Magians' jizya, and 'Umar and the plague. Read the narration carefully.
Pay attention to the number of witnesses: in both cases, only 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf testified.
2
Identify the legal matters
The first matter is the permissibility of taking jizya from Magians. The second matter is the ruling on entering or leaving a plague-affected land.
Both are significant legal rulings without explicit Quranic text.
3
Analyze 'Umar's response
In both cases, 'Umar accepted the testimony of a single companion without asking for another witness. He implemented the ruling immediately.
Contrast with Abu Bakr's request for a second witness in the grandmother case.
4
Derive the usul principle
The principle is that a solitary report from a reliable companion is binding (hujja). The absence of a request for a second witness shows that such a request is not required; it is a matter of discretion (ihtiyat).
This strengthens the Hanafi stance that ahad hadith are valid proofs.

So he said: Bring a witness with you on that. So this is better for precaution, and one suffices. Do you not see that 'Umar accepted the testimony of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf alone when he testified before him that the Messenger of God mentioned the Magians in his presence and said: Treat them according to the practice of the People of the Book in taking the kharaj. So 'Umar accepted his statement alone. And 'Umar accepted the statement of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf concerning the plague when he wanted to enter Syria while the plague was there. So 'Umar consulted about entering, and some of the Muhajirun indicated entering, and Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah said: O Commander of the Faithful, do you flee from the decree of God? And some people from Mecca said: Do not enter. Then 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf came and said: I heard the Messenger of God say: 'When this affliction occurs in a land, do not enter it; and when it occurs
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage provides extensive historical evidence that the Companions (Sahaba) of the Prophet ﷺ acted upon isolated hadith (akhbar al-ahad). The author first notes that such examples are too numerous and too famous to require exhaustive enumeration. The core argument is: if the Sahaba themselves accepted single-person reports in matters of law (such as inheritance, jizya, and waiting period), then later scholars are justified in doing so. The examples cited include: (1) Abu Bakr accepting the testimony of Mughira ibn Shu'ba and Muhammad ibn Maslama regarding the grandmother's sixth share, initially requiring two witnesses but ultimately acting upon a single report combined with another; (2) 'Umar accepting the testimony of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf alone regarding the Magians' jizya; (3) 'Umar accepting 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf's solitary report about the plague and the prohibition of entering an afflicted land. Additionally, the story of Furay'a bint Malik illustrates the Prophet's own ruling based on a single narration. All these examples demonstrate that the Sahaba did not require multiple witnesses or chains for every report; rather, they acted upon ahad hadith when they deemed them reliable. The footnote from Kitab al-Istihsan further elaborates that 'Umar's request for additional witnesses was for precaution (ihtiyat), not because a single witness was insufficient. Thus, the Hanafi position that ahad hadith are valid and binding is firmly rooted in the practice of the early community.

Key Terms
الآحاد (al-ahad): isolated or single-narrator hadith, as opposed to mutawatir
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): a method of legal reasoning preferring a ruling due to equity or necessity, often based on a hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did 'Umar accept 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf's testimony about the Magians without asking for a second witness?
A) Because he considered 'Abd al-Rahman infallible.
B) Because he accepted solitary testimony as sufficient proof.
C) Because he had already known the ruling.
D) Because the matter was trivial.

Key Takeaway
'Umar's acceptance of solitary testimony from 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf demonstrates the Hanafi principle that a single reliable narrator's report is binding proof in legal matters.

Companions' Practice as Proof for Khabar al-Wahid
Demonstration of khabar al-wahid acceptance through Companions' actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the practice of the Companions (Abu Bakr, Umar, Ali, and others) as proof for the acceptance of khabar al-wahid in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The Companions' consistent acceptance of single reporters establishes khabar al-wahid as binding.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi presents a wealth of examples from the early Caliphs and the Prophet himself showing the acceptance of solitary reports. Before the rational and consensus proofs, the author grounds the authority of khabar al-wahid in the living practice of the Sahaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples
List the Companions cited (Abu Bakr, Umar, Ali) and the situations where they accepted a single report. Note the specific cases: grandmother's inheritance, permission to enter, Magians, plague, blood money, sending Dihya to Caesar, Ali's oath, breaking wine jars.
Focus on the pattern: each case involves a single reporter being sufficient for action.
2
Extract the principle
What common feature makes these examples relevant? All involve acceptance of a single report without requiring multiple witnesses or tawatur. The principle is that khabar al-wahid from a trustworthy narrator is actionable.
The author uses 'ألا ترى' to highlight the reasoning.
3
Analyze the authority
Why is the Companions' practice considered a proof (dalil) in usul? It reflects either direct prophetic practice (e.g., the Prophet sending Dihya alone) or tacit approval (the Prophet did not rebuke these actions).
This is a form of sunnah taqririyyah or ijma sukuti.
4
Connect to Hanafi usul
How does this support the rule that khabar al-wahid is hujja? These examples show that the early community, closest to revelation, acted on solitary reports, establishing a precedent that later Hanafi usulis codified.
The Hanafi school uses these as evidence that khabar al-wahid is binding in legal matters.

ذكر محمد رحمه الله في هذا غير حديث في كتاب الاستحسان)، ألا ترى أن أبا بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه شهد عنده المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعطى الجدةَ أم الأم السدس ، فقال : ائتِ بشاهد آخر، فجاء محمد بن مسلمة، فشهد على مثل شهادته، فأعطى أبو بكر الجدة السدس. وعمر شهد عنده أبو موسى أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «إذا استأذن أحدكم ثلاثاً، فلم يُؤذن له فليرجع ، فقال : ائتِ معك بشاهد على ذلك. فهذا أفضل للاحتياط والواحد يجزىء، ألا ترى أن عمر قبل شهادة عبد الرحمن بن عوف، شهد عنده وحده أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذكر عنده المجوس، فقال: سنوا بهم سنة أهل الكتاب في أخذ الخراج، فأجاز عمر قوله وحده. وأجاز عمر قول عبد الرحمن بن عوف في الطاعون حين أراد أن يدخل الشام، وكان بها الطاعون، فاستشار عمرُ في الدخول، فأشار إليه بعض المهاجرين بالدخول، وقال أبو عبيدة بن الجراح : يا أمير المؤمنين أتفر من قدر الله؟! وقال قوم من أهل مكة: لا تدخل، فجاء عبد الرحمن بن عوف فقال : إني سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «إذا وقع هذا الرجزُ بأَرض : فلا تدخلوا عليه، وإذا وقع وأنتم بها فلا تخرجوا منها»، وأخذ عمر بقوله. وحديث آخر: أراد عمر بن الخطاب أن لا يورث امرأة من دية زوجها شيئاً، حتى شهد له الضحاك بن سفيان أن كتاب رسولِ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتاه أن تُورَّتْ امرأةُ أَشيَم الضبابي من دية زوجها أشيم»، فأخذ بقوله. وبعث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم دحية الكلبي وحده إلى قيصر ملك الروم بكتابه، يدعوه إلى الإسلام»، فكان حجةً عليه. وقال علي بن أبي طالب: كنتُ إذا لم أسمع من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فحدثني به غيره استحلفتُه على ذلك، وحدثني به أبو بكر الصديق، وصدق أبو بكر. وبلغنا أن نفراً من أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كانوا يشربون شراباً لهم من الفَضيخ فأتاهم آت فأخبرهم أن الخمر قد حرمت، فقال أبو طلحة يا أنس قُم إلى هذه الجرار فاكسرها ، فقمتُ إليها فكسرتها حتى اهراق ما فيها». واختصرنا على هذه الجملة؛ لوضوحها واستفاضتها. وأجمعت الأمةُ على قبول أخبار الآحاد من الوكلاء والرُّسُل والمضاربين وغيرهم. وأما المعقولُ: فلأن الخبر يصير حجةً بصفة الصدق والخبرُ يحتمل الصدق والكذب وبالعدالة بعد أهلية الإخبار: يترجح الصدق، وبالفسق يترجح الكذبُ، فوجب العمل برجحان الصدق؛ ليصير حجةً. قال محمد: وحدثنا وكيع عن سفيان عن سماك عن عكرمة «أن أعرابياً شهد عند رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في رؤية الهلال قال أتشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأني رسول الله ؟ فقال : نعم فأمر الناس فصاموا»، هذا حاصل ما فيه، والله أعلم.
Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in this [book] other than a hadith in the Book of Istihsan. Do you not see that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) was testified before by al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave the grandmother, the mother of the mother, one-sixth. So he said: Bring another witness. Then Muhammad ibn Maslama came and testified similarly, so Abu Bakr gave the grandmother one-sixth. And Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) was testified before by Abu Musa that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you seeks permission three times and is not given permission, then let him return.' He said: Bring a witness with you on that. This is better for caution, but one [witness] suffices. Do you not see that Umar accepted the testimony of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf alone when he testified that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) mentioned the Magians and said: 'Treat them like the People of the Book in taking the jizya.' So Umar accepted his statement alone. And Umar accepted the statement of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf regarding the plague when he wanted to enter Syria, and there was plague there. Umar consulted about entering. Some of the Muhajirun suggested entering, and Abu Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah said: O Commander of the Faithful, do you flee from the decree of Allah? A group from Mecca said: Do not enter. Then Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf came and said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'When this affliction occurs in a land, do not enter it, and when it occurs while you are in it, do not leave it.' So Umar accepted his statement. And another hadith: Umar ibn al-Khattab wanted not to give a woman anything from the blood money of her husband until al-Dahhak ibn Sufyan testified to him that the book of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) came to him that the wife of Ashyam al-Dhubabi should be given inheritance from the blood money of her husband Ashyam. So he accepted his statement. And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent Dihya al-Kalbi alone to Caesar, the king of the Romans, with his letter calling him to Islam, so it was a proof against him. And Ali ibn Abi Talib said: If I did not hear from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and someone else narrated it to me, I would make him swear by that, and Abu Bakr al-Siddiq narrated it to me, and I trusted Abu Bakr. And it reached us that a group of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) were drinking a beverage they had from fadikh, and a person came and informed them that wine had been prohibited. So Abu Talha said: O Anas, stand up and break these jars. So I stood up and broke them until what was in them spilled out. And we have limited [our discussion] to this summary due to its clarity and widespread nature. And the Ummah has agreed on accepting reports of individuals from agents, messengers, mudaribs, and others. As for the rational [proof]: because a report becomes a proof by the attribute of truthfulness, and a report can be true or false. By [the narrator's] justice after the eligibility of reporting, truthfulness preponderates, and by his sinfulness falsehood preponderates. So it is necessary to act on the preponderance of truthfulness to make it a proof. Muhammad said: And Waki' narrated to us from Sufyan from Simak from Ikrima that a Bedouin testified before the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) regarding sighting the crescent. He said: Do you testify that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah? He said: Yes. So he ordered the people to fast. This is the summary of what is in it, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi provides extensive proof for the acceptance of khabar al-wahid (solitary narration) in Hanafi usul. The author begins by citing multiple examples from the early Caliphs: Abu Bakr accepting the testimony of two witnesses for the grandmother's inheritance, Umar initially requiring two witnesses but also accepting single witnesses (like Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf on the plague and Magians), and the Prophet sending Dihya al-Kalbi alone to the Roman emperor. Ali's practice of requiring an oath from single narrators except when Abu Bakr reported is also mentioned. Then the Companions accepted the news of wine prohibition from a single informer. The author then summarizes that the Ummah has agreed (ijma) on accepting reports of individuals in matters of agency and messengers. He then presents a rational argument (al-ma'qul): a report becomes a proof because truthfulness preponderates over falsehood when the narrator is just, so acting on that preponderance is obligatory. Finally, he relates a hadith from Muhammad al-Shaybani about a Bedouin whose testimony about the crescent was accepted by the Prophet. All these proofs together establish that khabar al-wahid from a trustworthy narrator is a binding legal evidence (hujja) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report narrated by one or two individuals that does not reach the level of tawatur.
عدالة (adala): The quality of being upright and just, causing truthfulness to preponderate in reports.
حجة (hujja): A proof or binding evidence that necessitates action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Umar
C) Ali
D) Abu Talha

Key Takeaway
The practice of the Companions, as shown in multiple examples, demonstrates that khabar al-wahid from a trustworthy narrator is a binding proof in the Hanafi school.

Rational and Ijma Proofs for Khabar al-Wahid
Rational and ijma proofs for khabar al-wahid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rational argument (al-ma'qul) and the consensus (ijma) as proofs for the binding nature of khabar al-wahid in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Rational preponderance of truthfulness and the consensus of the Ummah make khabar al-wahid a binding proof.

After presenting the practice of the Companions, al-Bazdawi provides two additional proofs: the rational argument that justice preponderates truthfulness, and the consensus of the Ummah on accepting solitary reports in transactions. These proofs solidify the authority of khabar al-wahid in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational argument
Read the text: 'وأما المعقول: فلأن الخبر يصير حجةً بصفة الصدق... بالعدالة يترجح الصدق'. This is a qiyas: the asl is the obligation to follow truth when preponderant, the far' is khabar al-wahid, and the illa is the preponderance of truth due to justice.
The Hanafis consider this a qati' argument, meaning it is definitive.
2
Identify the consensus
Find the sentence: 'وأجمعت الأمةُ على قبول أخبار الآحاد من الوكلاء والرُّسُل والمضاربين وغيرهم.' This ijma covers transactions, demonstrating that solitary reports are accepted by all schools in practical dealings.
Note that the consensus is specifically about reports from agents, messengers, etc., but it is used analogically for all khabar al-wahid.
3
Relate to the hadith
The Bedouin crescent sighting hadith is a direct example from the Prophet accepting a single testimony. This serves as both a naqli and aqli proof (the Prophet's action confirms the rational principle).
This hadith is cited as a supporting evidence, not a primary proof.
4
Connect the proofs
How do these proofs complement each other? The rational argument provides a logical basis, the ijma provides historical consensus, and the hadith provides prophetic precedent. Together they make khabar al-wahid a multi-layered proof.
In Hanafi usul, multiple proofs converge to establish certainty.

Source Text

All these proofs together establish that khabar al-wahid from a trustworthy narrator is a binding legal evidence (hujja) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report narrated by one or two individuals that does not reach the level of tawatur.
عدالة (adala): The quality of being upright and just, causing truthfulness to preponderate in reports.
حجة (hujja): A proof or binding evidence that necessitates action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rational argument in the passage, why does a report from a just narrator become a proof?
A) Because the narrator is infallible
B) Because truthfulness preponderates over falsehood due to justice
C) Because two witnesses are always more reliable
D) Because the report is verified by multiple chains

Key Takeaway
The rational argument (preponderance of truth through justice) and the consensus of the Ummah provide two strong proofs that khabar al-wahid is a binding legal evidence in the Hanafi school.

Epistemological Status of Khabar al-Wahid: Preponderant Opinion
Establishes that khabar al-wahid yields preponderant opinion sufficient for action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why khabar al-wahid yields ghalib al-ra'y and not ilm al-yaqin, and apply this principle to classify the epistemic weight of reports in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid provides preponderant opinion, sufficient for action but not certainty.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi lays the Hanafi foundation for the authority of single-report hadith. Imam al-Bazdawi argues that khabar al-wahid cannot produce certain knowledge due to inherent probability, yet it is a valid proof for action because the truthfulness of an 'adl narrator outweighs possible falsehood. Understanding this epistemic hierarchy is essential for distinguishing between levels of evidence in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that khabar al-wahid yields ghalib al-ra'y, not yaqin. The author explicitly says it provides 'ilm ghalib al-ra'y.
Key phrase: 'فهذا الخبر من العدل يفيد علماً بغالب الرأي'.
2
Examine the rational proof (dalil aqli)
Trace the argument: a report becomes a hujja (proof) because truthfulness is preponderant over falsehood due to the narrator's 'adala. Action on preponderance is wajib.
This is a qiyas jaliy: acting on preponderance is required in other legal contexts.
3
Understand the analogy
The author analogizes to qiyas and judicial evidence (bayyinat): both are valid despite lacking certainty. This shows that action without yaqin is acceptable in fiqh.
Analogy reinforces that khabar al-wahid suffices for 'amal.
4
Note the refutation of yaqin
The author forcefully rejects any claim that khabar al-wahid gives certainty. Because probability (ihtimal) always remains, certainty is impossible.
Strong language: 'باطل بلا شبهة' and 'من أنكر هذا فقد سفه نفسه'.
5
Recognize the exception: accumulation to tawatur
When ahad reports accumulate to the level of tawatur, certainty arises. This is an emergent property, like ijma removing doubt.
This sets the boundary: khabar al-wahid alone = zanni; tawatur = qat'i.

And we summarized this sentence due to its clarity and prevalence. And the Ummah has unanimously accepted the reports of single individuals from agents, messengers, partners, and others. As for the rational proof: because the report becomes a proof by the quality of truthfulness, and the report is subject to truthfulness and falsehood. By 'adala (integrity) after the eligibility to report, truthfulness is preponderant, and by fisq (immorality) falsehood is preponderant, so action based on the preponderance of truthfulness is obligatory so that it becomes a proof for action. And the possibility of forgetfulness and falsehood is considered due to the loss of certain knowledge, and this is because action is valid without certain knowledge. And this is because a single report is inevitably subject to probability, and there is no certainty with probability, and whoever denies this has belittled himself and led his intellect astray. Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi is a foundational statement on the authority of khabar al-wahid (single-report hadith) in Hanafi usul. It begins by claiming ijma' (consensus) of the Ummah on the acceptance of akhbar al-ahad, citing examples like agents, messengers, and business partners. Then it provides the rational proof: a report becomes a hujja (proof) when the truthfulness of the narrator is preponderant due to 'adala. Since falsehood is preponderant with fisq, we act on the preponderance of truth. The author then clarifies the epistemological status: khabar al-wahid does not give 'ilm al-yaqin (certain knowledge); it only yields 'ilm ghalib al-ra'y (knowledge of predominant opinion), which is sufficient for binding action. This is analogous to qiyas and judicial evidence, which also lack certainty. The author forcefully refutes any claim that khabar al-wahid produces yaqin, stating that anyone who claims otherwise has 'belittled himself'. Importantly, when multiple ahad reports accumulate to the level of tawatur, they produce certainty, similar to how ijma removes doubt. Finally, the passage discusses akhbar al-ahad in matters of the afterlife (ahkam al-akhira): some are well-known (mashhur), some are less, but they all provide a type of knowledge and also require action in the form of 'aqd al-qalb (heartfelt conviction). The supporting Quranic verses illustrate that knowledge can exist without external acceptance.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one individual or a small number not reaching the level of tawatur.
غالب الرأي (ghalib al-ra'y): Preponderant opinion, a level of knowledge that is not certain but outweighs its opposite, sufficient for legal action.
علم اليقين (ilm al-yaqin): Certain knowledge free from any possibility of error, produced only by tawatur or direct observation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) They become invalid because of contradiction.
D) They are only valid for matters of the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid provides preponderant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y), sufficient for legal action but not certainty; only tawatur yields ilm al-yaqin.

Epistemological Value of Khabar al-Wahid: Predominant Opinion, Not Certainty
Establishes that khabar al-wahid yields predominant opinion, not certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that khabar al-wahid yields ghalib al-ra'y (predominant opinion) for action, and distinguish it from the certainty required for belief.

Core Idea
Khabar al-wahid provides predominant opinion sufficient for action, not certainty; certainty requires tawatur.

In Hanafi usul, the epistemological weight of a solitary report is a foundational issue that affects how we derive rulings. Al-Bazdawi, in his work Kanz al-Wusul, argues forcefully that khabar al-wahid does not produce certainty (yaqin) but rather predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y), which is legally sufficient for action. He supports this with analogies from qiyas and judicial evidence, and distinguishes the requirement for matters of the afterlife, where heart's conviction (aqd) is needed. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the first few lines to find al-Bazdawi's assertion about what khabar al-wahid does and does not provide. Look for the terms 'علم اليقين' and 'غالب الرأي'.
The claim is that khabar al-wahid provides knowledge by predominant opinion, not certainty.
2
Trace the analogies
Notice how the author compares khabar al-wahid to qiyas and judicial evidence (bayyinat). Identify the common thread: all three are valid for action without certainty.
The analogy shows that the Hanafi school consistently applies a standard of predominant opinion for practical rulings.
3
Understand the limit of khabar al-wahid
Find the passage where al-Bazdawi explains why khabar al-wahid cannot give certainty: because probability (ihtimal) remains. Contrast this with tawatur, which removes doubt.
The key phrase: 'لا يقين مع الاحتمال' (no certainty with probability).
4
Differentiate matters of the afterlife
Examine the section starting with 'فأما الآحاد في أحكام الآخرة'. Identify the type of response required (aqd al-qalb) and why it differs from action-based rulings.
Heart's conviction is above mere knowledge and is needed for belief-related reports.
5
Note the consequence for naskh
Read the concluding sentence about permitting discussion of abrogation before action. Consider why this follows from the epistemological status.
Since khabar al-wahid is a valid proof (hujja), it can be used to argue for naskh even before practical implementation.

Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the epistemological status of khabar al-wahid (solitary report) in Hanafi usul. The author, al-Bazdawi, argues that khabar al-wahid does not produce certainty (yaqin) but rather predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y), which is sufficient for action. He supports this by analogy with qiyas and judicial evidence, which also rely on predominant opinion. He refutes the claim of certainty by noting that even mashhur reports (famous but not mutawatir) do not give certainty, so a solitary report is even less. Then he discusses that when ahad reports aggregate to the level of tawatur, they become certain—similar to how scholarly consensus removes doubt. For matters of the afterlife, he distinguishes that khabar al-wahid can produce a type of knowledge requiring heart's conviction (aqd), which is above mere cognition. He quotes two Quranic verses to illustrate that internal certainty can exist alongside external rejection (the enemies of the prophets knew the truth but denied it). Finally, he concludes that since khabar al-wahid is a valid proof, we now proceed to classify the narrators. This passage establishes the Hanafi position that khabar al-wahid is acceptable for establishing legal obligations in this world (actions) but not for matters requiring absolute certainty, except through tawatur. Notably, the author permits discussing naskh (abrogation) based on khabar al-wahid before action, a distinctive Hanafi view.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report transmitted by one or a few narrators that does not reach the level of tawatur.
علم اليقين (ilm al-yaqin): Certain knowledge free from any possibility of error.
غالب الرأي (ghalib al-ra'y): Predominant opinion, a level of probability that preponderates over doubt and is sufficient for practical obligation.
عقد القلب (aqd al-qalb): Heart's conviction, a firm internal assent that transcends mere knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y)
C) Conjecture (wahm) with no legal value
D) Certainty after aggregation by multiple chains

Key Takeaway
Khabar al-wahid is epistemologically zanni, providing predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y) sufficient for action, but not certainty; certainty requires tawatur.

Classification of Narrators in Khabr al-Wahid
Classification of narrators for khabr al-wahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two main categories of narrators for khabr al-wahid according to Hanafi usul and list the subtypes of known and unknown narrators.

Core Idea
The reliability of a narrator is judged by their fiqh and the reaction of trusted critics.

This passage transitions from the discussion of abrogation to a fundamental classification in usul al-fiqh: how to assess narrators of solitary reports (khabr al-wahid). The Hanafi school divides narrators into known (ma'ruf) and unknown (majhul), with further distinctions that determine whether their reports are accepted as legal proofs.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two main categories
Identify that narrators are either known (ma'ruf) or unknown (majhul).
2
Differentiate the two types of known narrators
Distinguish between a narrator known for fiqh and ijtihad (type 1) and one known only for hadith transmission (type 2).
3
Enumerate the five aspects of unknown narrators
List the five scenarios: thiqat narrate and act, thiqat remain silent, thiqat oppose, thiqat differ, and hadith not among the Salaf.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Consider the given examples of known narrators (caliphs, Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, etc.) and understand why they are considered ma'ruf.

Therefore, we allowed the statement of abrogation before action and before the ability to act, and Allah. 19. Chapter: Division of the narrator whose report is made a proof. And it is two types: known and unknown. And the known are two kinds: 1 - whoever is known for fiqh and advancement in ijtihad. 2 - and whoever is known for narration, without fiqh and fatwa. As for the unknown, it has aspects: 1 - either that trustworthy narrators narrate from him and act upon his hadith and testify to the authenticity of the hadith. 2 - or they remain silent about criticizing him. 3 - or they oppose him with criticism and rejection. 4 - or they differ about him. 5 - or his hadith did not appear among the early predecessors. So the category of the unknown becomes five aspects. 1 - As for the known ones: the rightly guided caliphs, Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, Abdullah ibn Abbas, Abdullah ibn Umar, Zaid ibn Thabit, Muadh ibn Jabal, and Abu Musa.

Commentary
This passage begins with a concluding remark from the previous discussion on abrogation (naskh), stating that abrogation is permissible before action, analogous to physical acts. Then the text transitions to a new chapter on the classification of narrators whose single reports (khabr al-wahid) serve as legal proofs. The narrator is divided into two broad categories: known (ma'ruf) and unknown (majhul). The known narrator is of two types: (1) one recognized for deep fiqh and advanced ijtihad, and (2) one recognized merely for hadith transmission without juristic expertise. The unknown narrator has five possible scenarios: (1) trustworthy narrators report from him, act on his hadith, and attest to its authenticity; (2) they remain silent without criticizing him; (3) they openly criticize and reject him; (4) they differ among themselves about him; (5) his hadith was not circulated among the early Salaf. The author then lists examples of known narrators: the four rightly guided caliphs and prominent companions like Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Umar, Zaid ibn Thabit, Muadh ibn Jabal, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The text cuts off here, but it establishes the hierarchy of reliability for accepting khabr al-wahid. Hanafi scholars give weight to the first type of known narrator (those with fiqh), as their reports are more likely to be accurate. The unknown narrator's report is generally rejected unless thiqat act upon it, indicating implicit acceptance.

Key Terms
خبر الواحد (khabr al-wahid): A report narrated by a single person or a few individuals that does not reach the level of tawatur.
معروف (ma'ruf): A narrator known for reliability, either as a jurist (faqih) or as a mere transmitter (rawi).
مجهول (majhul): A narrator whose reliability is unknown; his report is not accepted unless thiqat act upon it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of unknown narrators (majhul) are there?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of narrators into known (ma'ruf) and unknown (majhul) with their subtypes is essential for determining the probative value of a khabr al-wahid.

Hanafi Classification of Narrators and Authority over Qiyas
Classification of narrators and authority over qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify narrators according to the Hanafi school and apply the rule for when a narrator's report takes precedence over qiyas.

Core Idea
A narrator's juristic knowledge determines whether his report overrides qiyas or not.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi establishes a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: not all narrators are equal when it comes to the authority of their reports over analogical reasoning (qiyas). The author divides narrators into known and unknown categories, and then within the known, distinguishes between those who are jurists and those who are merely reliable transmitters. This distinction has practical consequences for how conflicting evidence is resolved, as illustrated by the dispute with Imam Malik over the priority of a jurist-narrator's hadith versus qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of narrators
Read the opening sentence: the narrator whose report is authoritative (hujja) is of two types: known (ma'rūf) and unknown (majhūl). Note that 'known' here implies recognition in the scholarly community, not just by name.
The Hanafi school requires the narrator to be 'known' for acceptance, unlike other schools that only require general reliability.
2
Distinguish the two subtypes of known narrators
Within 'known', there are two kinds: (1) those known for jurisprudence (fiqh) and ijtihad, and (2) those known only for narration (riwāya) without juristic expertise. List the examples given: the first group includes the Rightly Guided Caliphs and prominent Companions like Ibn Mas'ud; the second includes Abu Huraira, Anas, and Bilal.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school and affects whether the report can override qiyas.
3
Understand the ruling for each subtype
For the first group (jurist-narrators): their hadith is authoritative whether it agrees with qiyas or opposes it. For the second group (non-jurist narrators): if their hadith agrees with qiyas, it is accepted; if it opposes, it is set aside unless there is no room for opinion (i.e., qiyas is impossible).
The reason given is that non-jurist narrators might misunderstand the Prophet's words due to transmission by meaning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi-Maliki dispute
The author presents a reported view of Imam Malik that qiyas should be preferred over the hadith of a jurist-narrator due to alleged doubt in the hadith's continuity. Identify the Hanafi response: the hadith is certain in its origin, any doubt is in transmission, while qiyas is inherently probable. The report is self-explanatory, while qiyas requires inference.
The Hanafi response uses the analogy: hearing (sama') is superior to opinion (ra'y) in correctness.
5
Apply the principle to a practical case
The text gives the example of determining the qibla direction: a single narrator's report (khabar al-wāhid) about the qibla takes precedence over personal ijtihad (taḥarrī). This illustrates that a jurist-narrator's report can override reasoned opinion.
This is a concrete thamarah al-khilaf: the practical difference between the Hanafi and Maliki positions.

باب تقسيم الراوي الذي جعل خبره حجة وهو ضربان: معروف، ومجهول. والمعروف نوعان: ۱ - مَن عُرف بالفقه والتقدم في الاجتهاد. ۲ - ومَن عُرف بالرواية، دون الفقه والفتيا. وأما المجهول : فعلى وجوه ١ - إما أن يروي عنه الثقاتُ ويَعملوا بحديثه، ويشهدوا له بصحة الحديث. ٢ ـ أو يسكتوا عن الطعن فيه. ٣- أو يعارضوه بالطعن والرد. ٤ـ أو اختلف فيه. ه أو لم يظهر حديثه بين السلف. فصار قسم المجهول على خمسة أوجه. ١ - أما المعروفون فالخلفاء الراشدون، وعبد الله بن مسعود وعبد الله ابن عباس وعبد الله بن عمر وزيد بن ثابت ومعاذ بن جبل وأبو موسى الأشعري وعائشة رضي الله عنهم، وغيرهم ممن اشتهر بالفقه والنظر. وحديثهم حجة، خالفه القياس أو وافقه. وقال مالك رحمه الله فيما يُحكى عنه : بل القياس مقدم عليه؛ لأن القياس حجةٌ بإجماع السلف وفي اتصال هذا الحديث شبهة. والجواب : أن الخبر يقين بأصله، وإنما دخلت الشبهةُ في نقله. والرأي محتمل بأصله في كل وصف على الخصوص، فكان الاحتمال في الرأي أصلاً، وفي الحديث عارضاً. ولأن الوصف في النص كالخبر والرأي والنظر فيه كالسماع، والقياس عمل به، والوصف ساكت عن البيان، والخبر بيان بنفسه، فكان الخبرُ فوق الوصف في الإبانة، والسماع فوق الرأي في الإصابة. ولهذا قدَّمنا خبر الواحد على التحري في باب القبلة، فلا يجوز التحري معه. ٢- وأما روايةُ مَن لم يُعرف بالفقه، ولكنه معروف بالعدالة والحفظ والضبط، مثل أبي هريرة وأنس بن مالك وبلال رضي الله عنهم : فإن وافق القياس : عُمل به وإن خالفه لم يُترك إلا بالضرورة وانسدادِ باب الرأي. ووجه ذلك : أن ضبط حديث النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عظيم الخطر، وقد كان النقلُ بالمعنى مستفيضاً فيهم، فإذا قَصُرَ فقه الراوي عن دَرْك معاني حديث النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام ، وإحاطتها : لم يُؤمَن من أن
Section on the classification of the narrator whose report is made authoritative. And it is of two types: known and unknown. The known is of two types: 1. One who is known for jurisprudence and advancement in ijtihad. 2. And one who is known for narration, without jurisprudence and giving fatwa. As for the unknown, it is of several forms: 1. Either reliable narrators narrate from him and act upon his hadith, and testify for him to the authenticity of the hadith. 2. Or they remain silent about disparaging him. 3. Or they oppose him with disparagement and rejection. 4. Or they differ about him. 5. Or his hadith did not appear among the predecessors. Thus the division of the unknown becomes five aspects. 1. As for the known, they are the Rightly Guided Caliphs, and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, Abdullah ibn Abbas, Abdullah ibn Umar, Zaid ibn Thabit, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them, and others who are famous for jurisprudence and deliberation. Their hadith is authoritative, whether qiyas opposes it or agrees with it. And Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, said, as reported from him: Rather, qiyas is given precedence over it, because qiyas is authoritative by consensus of the predecessors, and in the continuity of this hadith there is doubt. The answer is: The report is certain in its origin, but doubt entered into its transmission. And opinion is probable in its origin in every specific attribute, so probability is original in opinion, and accidental in hadith. And because the attribute in the text is like the report, and opinion and consideration in it are like hearing, and qiyas is acted upon, and the attribute is silent about clarification, while the report is a clarification by itself. So the report is above the attribute in clarification, and hearing is above opinion in correctness. For this reason, we prefer the report of a single narrator over ijtihad in the chapter of qibla, so ijtihad is not permissible with it. 2. As for the narration of one who is not known for jurisprudence, but is known for integrity, memory and precision, such as Abu Huraira, Anas ibn Malik, and Bilal, may Allah be pleased with them: if it agrees with qiyas, it is acted upon; and if it opposes it, it is not abandoned except by necessity and closure of the door of opinion. The reason for that is that preserving the hadith of the Prophet, peace be upon him, is of great danger, and transmission by meaning was widespread among them. So if the narrator's jurisprudence falls short of comprehending the meanings of the hadith of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and encompassing them, it is not safe from
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi addresses the classification of narrators whose reports are considered authoritative (hujja) in the Hanafi school. The author divides narrators into two main categories: known (ma'rūf) and unknown (majhūl). The known narrators are further subdivided into two types: (1) those recognized for their deep jurisprudential knowledge (fiqh) and ijtihad, and (2) those known only for their reliable narration (riwāya) without being jurists. The unknown narrators fall into five subcategories based on how other reliable narrators treat their reports: acting upon them, remaining silent, opposing, differing, or the report not being known among the early generations. The author then discusses the ruling of the reports of the first type of known narrators (the jurists), mentioning examples like the Rightly Guided Caliphs and prominent Companions such as Ibn Mas'ud. Their hadith is accepted as authoritative regardless of whether it agrees or opposes qiyas. The author then engages in a polemic against a reported view of Imam Malik, who is said to give precedence to qiyas over such a hadith due to alleged doubt in its continuity. The Hanafi response defends the hadith's certainty in origin, arguing that any doubt only affects transmission, whereas opinion is inherently probable. The report is superior to qiyas in clarity, analogous to hearing being superior to reasoned opinion. This is applied practically: in the chapter on qibla, a single narrator's report overrides personal ijtihad. For the second type of known narrators (those who are merely reliable transmitters without deep fiqh, like Abu Huraira), their report is accepted only if it agrees with qiyas; if it contradicts, it is set aside except when there is no room for opinion (i.e., when qiyas is impossible). The reason is that misunderstanding the Prophet's words due to transmission by meaning could lead to error.

Key Terms
راوي معروف (rāwī maʿrūf): الراوي الذي اشتهر إما بالفقه والاجتهاد أو بالرواية والعدالة
راوي مجهول (rāwī majhūl): الراوي الذي لم يظهر حاله عند أهل الحديث
قياس (qiyās): الحكم في الفرع بعلة من الأصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the ruling on the hadith of a narrator who is known for jurisprudence (like the Rightly Guided Caliphs) when it contradicts qiyas?
A) It is rejected in favor of qiyas.
B) It is accepted and qiyas is abandoned.
C) It is accepted only if it is mutawatir.
D) It is suspended until further evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes a jurist-narrator's report over qiyas unconditionally, while a non-jurist narrator's report is only accepted if it agrees with qiyas or if qiyas is impossible.

Hadith from Fiqh-Savvy vs Non-Fiqh Narrators: Conflict with Qiyas
Classifying narrators by fiqh knowledge and rule for accepting their hadith vs qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi treatment of hadith from fiqh-knowledgeable narrators and non-fiqh narrators when they conflict with qiyas, and apply the rule to both categories.

Core Idea
A narrator's fiqh knowledge determines whether his hadith overrules qiyas or is only accepted if qiyas is blocked.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the reliability of a hadith is not solely based on the chain's integrity but also on the narrator's comprehension of Prophetic intent. Imam al-Bazdawi presents a nuanced rule: narrators famous for fiqh (like Aisha) have their hadith prioritized over qiyas, while narrators reliable but less versed in fiqh (like Abu Huraira) require caution. This lesson unpacks the classification, the Maliki objection, and the rationale behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrator categories
Note the two groups: (1) narrators known for fiqh (al-Ash'ari, Aisha) and (2) narrators reliable but not known for fiqh (Abu Huraira, Anas, Bilal). Each has a different rule when their hadith contradicts qiyas.
Fiqh knowledge here means understanding of legal implications, not just memory.
2
State the rule for fiqh narrators
For the first group, their hadith is a proof, and if it contradicts qiyas, qiyas is abandoned in favor of the hadith. This is the Hanafi position.
Imam Malik's opposing view is that qiyas is given precedence due to uncertainty in the hadith's transmission.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Malik
The Hanafi response argues that the hadith is certain in origin (thubut) while qiyas is inherently probabilistic. Uncertainty in transmission is secondary. The hadith is like hearing (sam') which is superior to opinion (ra'y).
The example of the qibla is given: a hadith takes precedence over ijtihad.
4
State the rule for non-fiqh narrators
For the second group, if their hadith agrees with qiyas, it is accepted. If it contradicts, it is not automatically rejected but is set aside only if the door of qiyas is blocked (i.e., no valid analogy can be made).
The reason: lack of fiqh raises the possibility that the narrator missed some nuance, introducing doubt.
5
Understand the exception when qiyas is blocked
When qiyas is blocked, the hadith becomes so strong that it can abrogate the Quran and well-known Sunnah and oppose consensus. The example is the hadith of musarrat from Abu Huraira.
This is a rare case; normally such hadith is not used to abrogate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأشعري وعائشة رضي الله عنهم، وغيرهم ممن اشتهر بالفقه والنظر. وحديثهم حجة، خالفه: ترك القياس به. وقال مالك رحمه الله فيما يُحكى عنه: بل القياس مقدم عليه؛ لأن القياس حجةٌ بإجماع السلف وفي اتصال هذا الحديث شبهة. والجواب: أن الخبر يقين بأصله، وإنما دخلت الشبهةُ في نقله. والرأي محتمل بأصله في كل وصف على الخصوص، فكان الاحتمال في الرأي أصلاً، وفي الحديث عارضاً. ولأن الوصف في النص كالخبر والرأي والنظر فيه كالسماع، والقياس عمل به، والوصف ساكت عن البيان، والخبر بيان بنفسه، فكان الخبرُ فوق الوصف في الإبانة، والسماع فوق الرأي في الإصابة. ولهذا قدَّمنا خبر الواحد على التحري في باب القبلة، فلا يجوز التحري معه. وأما روايةُ مَن لم يُعرف بالفقه، ولكنه معروف بالعدالة والحفظ والضبط، مثل أبي هريرة وأنس بن مالك وبلال رضي الله عنهم: فإن وافق القياس: عُمل به وإن خالفه لم يُترك إلا بالضرورة وانسدادِ باب الرأي. ووجه ذلك: أن ضبط حديث النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام عظيم الخطر، وقد كان النقلُ بالمعنى مستفيضاً فيهم، فإذا قَصُرَ فقه الراوي عن دَرْك معاني حديث النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإحاطتها: لم يُؤمَن من أن يذهب عليه شيء من معانيه بنقله، فتدخله شبهةً زائدة يخلو عنها القياس، فيحتاط في مثله. وإنما نعني بما قلنا: قصوراً عند المقابلة بفقه الحديث، فأما الازدراء بهم: فمعاذ الله ذلك، فإن محمداً رحمه الله حكى عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه في غير موضع أنه احتج بمذهب أنس بن مالك رضي الله عنه، وقلده، فما ظنُّك في أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه؟! حتى إن المذهب عند أصحابنا رحمهم الله في ذلك: أنه لا يُرَدُّ حديث أمثالهم إلا إذا انسدَّ باب الرأي والقياس؛ لأنه إذا انسد: صار الحديث ناسخاً للكتاب، والحديث المشهور، ومعارِضاً للإجماع. وذلك مثل حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه في المصراة: أنه انسدَّ فيه باب الرأي، فصار ناسخاً للكتاب والسُّنَّةِ المعروفة، معارضاً للإجماع.
Al-Ash'ari and Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them, and others who were famous for jurisprudence and reflection. Their hadith is a proof; if it contradicts [analogy], leave analogy because of it. And Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, said in what is related from him: 'Rather, analogy is given precedence over it, because analogy is a proof by consensus of the predecessors, and in the chain of this hadith there is uncertainty.' The answer: That the report is certain in its origin, but uncertainty entered into its transmission. Opinion is inherently probable in every specific attribute, so probability in opinion is original, and in the hadith it is accidental. And because the attribute in the text is like the report, and opinion and consideration in it are like hearing, and analogy is action by it, and the attribute is silent about clarification, while the report clarifies by itself, so the report is above the attribute in clarity, and hearing is above opinion in correctness. For this reason we gave precedence to the solitary report over investigation in the direction of the qibla, so investigation is not permissible with it. And as for the narration of one not known for jurisprudence but known for integrity, memory, and precision, such as Abu Huraira, Anas ibn Malik, and Bilal, may Allah be pleased with them: if it agrees with analogy, it is acted upon; if it contradicts it, it is not abandoned except out of necessity and when the door of opinion is blocked. The reason for that: that preserving the hadith of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, is of great importance, and transmission by meaning was widespread among them. So if the juristic understanding of the narrator falls short of grasping and encompassing the meanings of the hadith of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, it is not safe that something of its meanings might escape him in his transmission, so an additional doubt enters which is absent from analogy, so caution is exercised in such matters. And by what we said we only mean shortcoming when compared to juristic understanding of the hadith; as for belittling them, Allah forbid! For Muhammad [al-Shaybani], may Allah have mercy on him, reported from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, in more than one place that he argued with the school of Anas ibn Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, and emulated him. So what do you think of Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him? Indeed, the position of our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, in that regard is that the hadith of such people is not rejected unless the door of opinion and analogy is blocked; because when it is blocked, the hadith becomes an abrogator of the Book and the well-known Sunnah, and opposing consensus. And that is like the hadith of Abu Huraira, may Allah be pleased with him, concerning the musarrat: the door of opinion was blocked in it, so it became an abrogator of the Book and the well-known Sunnah, opposing consensus.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh rule from Imam al-Bazdawi's matn, addressing the treatment of hadith when it conflicts with qiyas (analogical reasoning), depending on the narrator's fiqh proficiency. The author first states the rule for narrators known for fiqh (like al-Ash'ari and Aisha): their hadith is authoritative even if it contradicts qiyas, and qiyas is abandoned in favor of the hadith. He then reports Imam Malik's opposing view that qiyas takes precedence due to the perceived uncertainty in the hadith's transmission. The Hanafi response refutes this by arguing that the hadith text is certain in origin (thubut) while opinion/analogy is inherently probabilistic, and the uncertainty in transmission does not undermine the original certainty. The author uses an analogy: the attribute (description) in a text is like a report and hearing is superior to opinion, so hadith (hearing) outranks qiyas (opinion). This is exemplified by the rule that a solitary hadith (khabar wahid) takes precedence over ijtihad in determining the qibla direction. Then the passage turns to the second category: narrators who are reliable but not known for fiqh, such as Abu Huraira, Anas, and Bilal. For such narrators, if their hadith agrees with qiyas, it is accepted; if it contradicts qiyas, it is not automatically rejected but is only set aside if necessary (i.e., when the door of qiyas is completely blocked and no independent reasoning is possible). The reason is that these narrators transmitted by meaning, and their lack of deep fiqh knowledge raises the possibility that they might have missed some nuance, introducing an element of doubt not present in qiyas. However, this is not a belittling of these Companions—Abu Hanifa himself followed Anas's school in several matters. The Hanafi position is that such hadith is not rejected unless qiyas is impossible; in that case, the hadith even becomes so strong that it can abrogate the Quran and well-known Sunnah and contradict consensus. The example is the hadith of al-musarrat (the animal with milk retained in its udder for sale) from Abu Huraira, where qiyas was blocked, so the hadith operated as an abrogator. This passage clarifies the nuanced Hanafi hierarchy of evidence: textual (hadith) generally takes precedence over rational analogy, but with narrators of lesser fiqh, the ruling is more cautious.

Key Terms
راوي (rawi): The narrator of a hadith; classified here by their knowledge of fiqh.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive legal rulings from primary sources.
مصراة (musarrat): An animal whose milk has been retained in the udder for several days to deceive a buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, what is the ruling on a hadith from a reliable but non-fiqh narrator that contradicts qiyas?
A) It is always rejected.
B) It is accepted if qiyas is blocked.
C) It is always accepted.
D) It is accepted only if it agrees with qiyas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a narrator's fiqh knowledge determines whether his hadith overrules qiyas; for non-fiqh narrators, qiyas is given precedence unless it is impossible to apply.

Definition of the Majhool Narrator in Hanafi Usul
Defining the unknown narrator in hadith transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the majhool narrator according to Hanafi usul and apply the criterion to identify whether a narrator is majhool.

Core Idea
A narrator known only by one or two hadiths is majhool — his hadith is not accepted unless corroborated.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul defines a key term in hadith criticism: the majhool narrator. Understanding this definition is crucial for evaluating the reliability of hadith transmissions. We will dissect the criterion, apply it to examples, and explore its implications for the acceptance of hadith in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Identify the exact phrasing: 'لم يعرف إلا بحديث أو حديثين'. This sets the threshold for calling a narrator majhool.
Pay attention to the numerical limit: one or two hadiths.
2
Connect to Hanafi usul
Recognize that this definition is used as a tool to assess narrator credibility. In Hanafi usul, the hadith of a majhool narrator is not accepted as hujja (proof) unless supported by external evidence.
This is part of the broader system of jarh wa ta'dil.
3
Apply to examples
Consider the companions listed: Wabisa ibn Ma'bad, Salama ibn al-Muhabbiq, and Ma'qil ibn Sinan. They are known only through one or two hadiths, making them majhool by this definition.
Note that being a companion does not automatically remove majhool status.
4
Evaluate implications
Reflect on what this means for hadith acceptance: a hadith from a majhool narrator is not automatically rejected but requires corroboration from other sources or qara'in (contextual indicators).
This is a foundational rule in Hanafi hadith methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المجهول : فإنما نعني به المجهول في رواية الحديث، بأن لم يُعرَف إلا بحديث، أو حديثين مثل وابصة بن معبد، وسلمة بن المحبق ومعقل بن سنان).
And as for the unknown: we mean by it the unknown in narrating hadith, that he is not known except by one or two hadiths, like Wabisa ibn Ma'bad, Salama ibn al-Muhabbiq, and Ma'qil ibn Sinan.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'majhool' in the context of hadith narration. A majhool narrator is one who is not widely known and is only recognized through one or two hadiths. The examples given: Wabisa ibn Ma'bad, Salama ibn al-Muhabbiq, and Ma'qil ibn Sinan. These are narrators whose reliability is limited due to lack of extensive knowledge of their character and memory. In Hanafi usul, the hadith of such narrators is generally not accepted as authoritative unless corroborated by other evidence or supported by the practice of the companions. This definition is foundational for evaluating the authenticity of hadith.

Key Terms
المجهول (al-majhool): The unknown narrator; one who is not known except by one or two hadiths

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A narrator known only through one or two hadiths
C) A narrator who is not a companion
D) A narrator who has been criticized

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a majhool narrator is defined as one known by only one or two hadiths, and such narrators are not considered reliable unless corroborated.

Acceptance of Hadith from Narrators Attested by the Salaf and Disputed Narrators
Usul rule on accepting narrators transmitted by Salaf and disputed narrators

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting hadith from narrators transmitted by the Salaf and apply it to disputed narrators, identifying the proof (dalil) and the opposing Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Salaf's transmission and silence imply acceptance; Hanafis accept hadith even if opposed to qiyas.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul addresses a key Hanafi principle: when the early generation (Salaf) narrate from a narrator or remain silent after transmission, their hadith is accepted. The same applies if there is a dispute about the narrator but trustworthy narrators have transmitted from him. The passage cites the practice of Ibn Mas'ud acting on the hadith of Ma'qil ibn Sinan as evidence, and contrasts the Shafi'i objection based on qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two scenarios for accepting a narrator's hadith: (1) when the Salaf explicitly attest to his authenticity or remain silent after transmission; (2) when there is disagreement about him but trustworthy narrators transmit from him.
These are separate conditions with the same ruling in Hanafi usul.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For both scenarios, the Hanafi ruling is that the hadith is accepted and acted upon, just like the hadith of a well-known trustworthy narrator.
The text says 'فكذلك عندنا' (it is the same with us) for the second scenario.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The primary dalil is the action of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, who accepted and acted on the hadith of Ma'qil ibn Sinan regarding Barwa' bint Washiq, despite Ali's rejection. This shows that transmission by trustworthy narrators is sufficient.
Ibn Mas'ud's happiness upon hearing the hadith indicates his confidence in it.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i rejects this category because it contradicts qiyas (analogical reasoning). For him, a hadith that opposes a clear analogy is not acceptable unless it is mutawatir or well-known.
The Hanafi response, implicit in the passage, is that transmission by trustworthy narrators outweighs opposition to qiyas.
5
Derive the underlying usul principle
The principle is: if trustworthy narrators transmit a hadith from a narrator—even if the narrator is disputed—the hadith is accepted over qiyas. This reflects the Hanafi prioritization of transmitted (naqli) evidence over rational (aqli) evidence in this context.
This is consistent with the Hanafi maxim: 'al-akhdh bi al-hadith awla min al-qiyas' (acting on hadith is preferable to analogy).

Source Text
